a series of smart musical instruments that teach novices how to play using
an iOS device and can be "hacked" by more advanced musicians to augment
traditional functionality with bespoke settings. I designed all of the
education and teaching components, as well as a great deal of the hardware
functionality. We placed 2nd at Tech Crunch Disrupt (and we weren't
invested in by CrunchBase, so we weren't going to win :) ) and raised $500k
across 2 products on crowdfunding platforms. Another is Hackster.io
<http://t.sigopn03.com/e1t/c/5/f18dQhb0S7lC8dDMPbW2n0x6l2B9nMJN7t5XZsQsBhjW1qMyFK5wvHZnVRJgRK56dP4Wf34C7ZF02?t=http%3A%2F%2Fhackster.io%2F&si=5804247449337856&pi=2703ba34-6314-47f9-ceb4-b8283cf5bec6>,
a platform I guided the UX development and userbase growth for, which
recently got a contract from Microsoft to run their IoT hardware
prototyping community. At Touch Surgery, where I currently work, I have set
up all content and marketing pipelines, coordinated all press (including
coverage in the past 3 weeks alone on Bloomberg TV, the BBC, and in the
Scottish Sun), and implemented viral looping and growth hacking practices
that have doubled our userbase in the past 6 months and has lead to an
increase in engagement of over 500%.

I've also been published for my academic behavioural research, and found
out this past weekend that one of my papers has been nominated for the Best
Social Cognition Research Paper of 2014 by an international behavioural
psychology review board.

Hope this helps give an idea. You can also take a look at my LinkedIn for a
more complete list.

I realised I made that 20 minutes statistic a bit convoluted - to clarify:

20 minutes per user IS accurate. Seconds and milliseconds was in reference
to the basis of measurement we use to record time. If it had been in
milliseconds, it would have been 20 seconds, not minutes per user. But it
is seconds, and thus minutes.

measure time spent. Specifically for Julia, you, max and I had that
conversation about how 20 minutes per user sounded high a few months ago
and max said he thought it might be milliseconds - I've confirmed that it
is in fact measured in seconds, not milliseconds.

Hi Pam,

Sorry for the delay - I've been bouncing around from Marrakesh to London
and now I'm in San Francisco, so have been a little bit behind on my email.

It's a pleasure to meet you and I sincerely appreciate the feedback. It's
always wonderful to receive validation from medical professionals who
understand what we're aiming to accomplish by offering a free interactive
supplement to traditional surgical training. I perused some of the amazing
things your doing at CTTI and it truly does sound like a great initiative.

If you'd be interested in possible collaboration, I'd love to introduce you
to our Head of Marketing, Kim Parkins. He'd be happy to explain a bit about
the partnerships and licensing we participate in to sustain the development
our platform, as well as the incredible global usage we've seen thus far.
Additional, we both feel there are a lot of synergies around the research
we're doing to help improve surgeon's clinical aptitude pre-surgery,
whether they're in developed or third world countries, as well as our
ability to train surgeons in a formal way on FDA cleared products.

Funny side note, Duke is one of the universities we're partnered with to do
our initial research!

Best,

Matthew

Hi,

apply.

I have a great deal of experience in the arts, entertainment, and
technology industries. I studied at the University of Michigan, Ann Arbor,
where I received a degree in business with a speciality in Marketing (I
will likely return to school and do a PhD in Consumer Behaviour, and have
been published academically in the Journal of Social Psychology and the
Open Science Framework for my research).

I have worked in marketing roles for Car and Driver Magazine, Ghostly
International Records, Intua BeatMaker, Incident gTar, Hackster.io, and a
number of other organisations on a full time and freelance basis. I
currently run the marketing for Touch Surgery, a London-based medical
startup that has received well over 8 digits of funding, and has 200,000
users (predominantly surgeons and medical students) in every country on
earth. I am very well connected in the entertainment, arts, and technology
industries both professionally and personally.

I have a great deal of experience in content production and curation, viral
marketing, paid digital marketing, SEO, fundraising, product development,
growth hacking, and data analytics.

users in target segment). I raised a total of $2 million dollars in funding
from VCs and private investors in my time in Silicon Valley, and decided to
bring a bit of what I've learned to the London start up scene (and yes, I
do have work authorisation :) )

Before that, I helped launched the gTar, a hardware startup that placed 2nd
in Tech Crunch Disrupt and raised $350k on Kickstarter, the most of any
musical instrument ever on a crowdfund. I've freelanced or worked full time
for about 6 or 7 years as a content producer and digital marketer. I've
produced content for Car and Driver Magazine, Ghostly International
Records, Hackster.io, Incident gTar, Touch Surgery, and countless other
companies. I've included a few of the videos I've produced, which are
probably the highest production value work I've done.

I also have studied content curation and consumption during the time I
spent in academia at the University of Michigan and the University of
California - Berkeley, and have been published in the Journal of Open
Science Data and the Journal of Social Psychology for my work. I will
likely go back and do a PhD in Consumer Behaviour in a few years to push
societal knowledge of what drives people to consumer and enjoy specific
pieces of aestetic content and messaging. I've included a few links to
videos I've produced and attached my resume, and am more than happy to
include a larger portfolio of writing, music, photography, and "growth
hacking" (I really hate that term though) samples.

I'd love to jump on a call and discuss how I can help you guys!

Best,

Matthew

No urgency in buying the domain really. We might want to discuss a general
domain strategy though, since there are a bunch of new domain suffixes
being released in the coming months.

As per timelines and tracking, that is all fine.

Lets jump on a call in the next few days, confirm everything including how
i'll track everything, and then i'll get cracking


*Batch One:* $250
Delivery: 1 week (but not before Weds of next week)
-Port over to WordPress (improvement to the aesthetic will likely be
something that we need a bit of outside help on, as I am no wiz with
CSS/HTML. It'll still probably look better than it does now, but to get it
right it'll need a designer's touch). I'll also need support from Muthu on
this likely.

   - As long as it looks at least a little better than it does now and is
   as (preferably more) user friendly, with greater draws to the
   Calls-to-action (which can also be A/B tested over time), that’ll
be fine. Shouldn't
   be an issue. Hopefully there's WiFi on my flight to NYC and I can just
   knock it all out on the plane. It will be a bit rough at first, but will
   still perform better on SEO and tracking functionality than the existing
   Wix site, and it sounds like Clare should be able to take a crack through
   after and get everything up to spec.

-Incorporate clearer calls to action
-Bing sitemap implementation (I checked, it's currently not) hell yes lets
go!!

*Batch Two:* $150
Delivery: 3 weeks after payment for Batch One and previous work
Items:
-Fix Keywords - I'm pretty sure I said I would look over the work your SEO
guy did and retarget here and there (which will actually need to
potentially be completely redone depending on how Wix handles keywords, and
will definitely have to be redone when we move to WordPress) but my focus
was going to be the content of the pages themselves i.e. SEO copywriting.
Regardless, I did say I would and so nevermind the additional fee. However,
we also need to be careful not to overhaul your SEO all the time - you need
to test and see what works after the dust clears, then tweak. It's also
worthwhile to note that it's actually GOOD practice to draw SEO work out,
especially keyword changes, over a longer period of time, because Google's
spiders crawl your page more often as they see it as frequently updated. I
appreciate your willingness to essentially do this for free given our
misunderstanding. No problem, but I do need payment for the first batch of
SEO copywriting, meta details copywriting, and keyword targeting research.
I would not have offered to look over the keywords for free had I realised
the extent of the issues, and updating the keyword stuff was outside of the
scope of what I was charging for, as well as is a complete waste of time to
implement (which is why I haven't) given that we're porting over to
wordpress and will have to redo it all anyways. But basically, the first
thing I said I would do is done, and I can't really justify moving forward
on other stuff until I receive payment.

-Request Info form - there's a fair bit of research that goes into this. In
marketing jargon, it's creating the off-ramp of the viral loop, and is
probably the most important part of your website. The implementation of
this will be rather quick, but what will be really important is optimising
as we collect enough data, and more importantly, setting up the channels to
collect that data effectively in the first place (i.e. A/B testing, the
traceable phone number, trackable email, etc.). I'll probably run a program
called Optimize.ly to A/B test this from the front end, and then will use
SPSS to analyse all of the data cumulatively (some of which will have to be
tabulated by clinic administrators). This process is quite easy for me and
will take little effort once I have it set up (i.e. analysing data and
tweaking copy/layout)

   - What channels would you be setting up to collect the data in the first
   place? Please clarify. Relatedly, you mention the traceable phone number
   and trackable email relating to this. However, wouldn’t those items fall
   under future SEM work and email marketing, instead of this batch? Without
   actually working specifically on either of those matters at the time of
   this batch, I don’t see see how these items are connected and from a labor
   perspective, what that might entail for you? Data Collection would be
   Google Analytics, testing would be Optimize.ly, MixPanel, and WordPress,
   analytics would be SPSS and Excel. Trackable phone number and email
   tracking protocol are instrumental to this data collection, so I included
   them in this step as opposed to SEM and email marketing (which SEM is more
   about the actually running of campaigns, and email marketing is more about
   B2C retention efforts (i.e. email newsletters, etc.)). If you want to
   understand exactly what this entails, I would go to one of the Consumer
   Behavior Marketing Professors at Vandy and ask them a little bit about
   their research and what the process entails. I learned what I know from
   Professor Katherine Burson at UofM and Professor Lief Nelson at UC Berkeley
   (and have been published for my work in 2 academic behavioral research
   journals), and this is likely a field I will end up doing a PhD in.
   Frankly, $150 for this alone is an absolute steal, let alone the entire
   batch, BUT it will be a very intangible sort of benefit. In that case,
   maybe it's best to leave this until last.
   - Please elaborate on what data would have to be tabulated by the clinic
   administrators? Is this data something they should already have/be
   tabulating? I am a little confused about this. Basically just a weekly
   or monthly report of how many people email, how many people call, and how
   they were referred. Without a serious commitment to tracking this data,
   they can't expect to be able to adequately track the results of my output
   in any way.
   - I know quite a bit about the off-ramp and viral loop, so no worries
   there.
   - I understand your quote a little bit better now that you are talking
   about A/B testing. This now sounds a bit more sophisticated and in-depth
   than simply adding a “request info form”, which the current Wix site has
   (and someone used this morning, in fact!).  Nice! But yeah, I mean I
   fucking A/B test my CVs, so I'm a bit of a wiz at it.
   - However, our *primary concern* about this patch and particularly this
   item is that it is based off of the assumption that we will have enough
   data to analyze. I highly doubt we will enough data for many months…and
   certainly not enough until at least a 5-6 weeks go by, I would think….that
   said, I’m not sure how we should adjust the proposal (totally open to your
   suggestions)…but I think the analysis should probably be moved to a
   later time, with just the implementation (which should not take long) at
   the time of this batch…and a corresponding price label So this was
   really getting at setting up the data collection points. Honestly, I'll be
   interested in the analysis for my own research purposes (my thing is
   studying the effect of aesthetic messaging), and it's such a long tailed
   activity that I'm not really going to charge you specifically for the
   analytics, more on
   - Would there be any additional costs relating to this batch in terms of
   optimize.ly or other software used for A/B testing? And if you do intend
   to use software in addition to optimze.ly, what might that be? Nope,
   everything I'd be using is free

*Batch Three:* $200
Delivery: 3 weeks after Batch Two
Items:
-FAQ Page (this is pricier because it actually entails creating a whole new
page (which thus includes content production/copywriting, layout design,
and more SEO), whereas if the employees flesh out their bio themselves, all
I have to do is copy/paste into an existing page)
-Testimonials - this takes a bit because of having to customise a carousel
(the rotating content thingies)

   - As long as there aren’t any additional costs from WP for
   running/adding a premium app to do this, we are OK here. Nope,
   everything I'd be using is free

-SEO Directories Part 1 - this is COMPLETELY different than SEO
optimisation. Optimisation deals with the text on your page - Directories
are basically ways of generating backlinks by submitting your info to
databases, creating accounts on appropriate sites, etc. This is really
about how much you want to put into this. I can't guarantee under any
circumstance the ability to be #1 on any specific keyword, but it's a hell
of a lot more likely if you hit SEO from every angle. This is basically
just the first step of link-building, which I'm more than happy to explain
a bit about via phone, but is way too much to type out. Part 1 would be the
main directories, Part Two would be more extended directories.

   - Please provide a list of part 1 directories you would submit to so we
   can better gauge the amount of work this could potentially entail, or
   propose what the deliverable would like like here. Finding the
   directories I would be submitting to would be a large part of the work I
   would be doing. The deliverable would likely be the tracking spreadsheet I
   use that would be a list of the directories I had or had not submitted to.
   - Please clarify what part 2 – more extended directories – entails. How
   is this different from part 1 directories? Can we not just combine them
   both into this batch, and perhaps add more content marketing and or SEM
   shit to batch 4? The returns to scale of this are pretty big as I will
   likely collect all the directories that would be viable in batch 3 and
   submit to the ones that are easiest/most important. However this is a
   process that will have a more significant impact on SEO if it's drawn out
   over time (as Google likes it when this isn't all done at once)

-Image SEO optimisation - this could take a ton of time if you have a lot
of photos, but I imagine you don't.

*Batch Four:* $100
Delivery: 3 weeks after Batch Three
Items:
-SEO Directories Part 2
-Public link-building (i.e. adding your pages as references on appropriate
wikipedia articles, etc.)
-Education page topic sentences

   - won’t have any questions/comments per this section until we get a
   revised proposal underway and responsibilities/deliverables are condensed,
   but overall this is a decent start for this batch. Not sure why the
   timeline is quite so long yet the price is relatively low, though… This
   stage is relatively easy. I could certainly put more time into it, but I
   figure this is the stage where we'll start some of the other content
   production, so I think it will work well that its's a bit more spread out
   here.

-Bing sitemap implementation (I checked, it's currently not)

*Batch Two:* $150
Delivery: 3 weeks after payment for Batch One and previous work
Items:
-Fix Keywords - I'm pretty sure I said I would look over the work your SEO
guy did and retarget here and there (which will actually need to
potentially be completely redone depending on how Wix handles keywords, and
will definitely have to be redone when we move to WordPress) but my focus
was going to be the content of the pages themselves i.e. SEO copywriting.
Regardless, I did say I would and so nevermind the additional fee. However,
we also need to be careful not to overhaul your SEO all the time - you need
to test and see what works after the dust clears, then tweak. It's also
worthwhile to note that it's actually GOOD practice to draw SEO work out,
especially keyword changes, over a longer period of time, because Google's
spiders crawl your page more often as they see it as frequently updated.
-Request Info form - there's a fair bit of research that goes into this. In
marketing jargon, it's creating the off-ramp of the viral loop, and is
probably the most important part of your website. The implementation of
this will be rather quick, but what will be really important is optimising
as we collect enough data, and more importantly, setting up the channels to
collect that data effectively in the first place (i.e. A/B testing, the
traceable phone number, trackable email, etc.). I'll probably run a program
called Optimize.ly to A/B test this from the front end, and then will use
SPSS to analyse all of the data cumulatively (some of which will have to be
tabulated by clinic administrators). This process is quite easy for me and
will take little effort once I have it set up (i.e. analysing data and
tweaking copy/layout)

*Batch Three:* $200
Delivery: 3 weeks after Batch Two
Items:
-FAQ Page (this is pricier because it actually entails creating a whole new
page (which thus includes content production/copywriting, layout design,
and more SEO), whereas if the employees flesh out their bio themselves, all
I have to do is copy/paste into an existing page)
-Testimonials - this takes a bit because of having to customise a carousel
(the rotating content thingies)
-SEO Directories Part 1 - this is COMPLETELY different than SEO
optimisation. Optimisation deals with the text on your page - Directories
are basically ways of generating backlinks by submitting your info to
databases, creating accounts on appropriate sites, etc. This is really
about how much you want to put into this. I can't guarantee under any
circumstance the ability to be #1 on any specific keyword, but it's a hell
of a lot more likely if you hit SEO from every angle. This is basically
just the first step of link-building, which I'm more than happy to explain
a bit about via phone, but is way too much to type out. Part 1 would be the
main directories, Part Two would be more extended directories.
-Image SEO optimisation - this could take a ton of time if you have a lot
of photos, but I imagine you don't.

*Batch Four:* $100
Delivery: 3 weeks after Batch Three
Items:
-SEO Directories Part 2
-Public link-building (i.e. adding your pages as references on appropriate
wikipedia articles, etc.)
-Education page topic sentences

Hi,

I'm reaching out iin regards to the Finance Assistant position you just
tweeted about. I have a great deal of experience in finance and music
business, LOVE the music on Warp, and think I could be a really fantastic
addition to your team.

I started working in music when I was quite young - from touring to
producing events to even being a staff member at a Rock n Roll summer camp.
At the University of Michigan Business School, I studied general business
with a concentration in Marketing and Entrepreneurship. On the side, I
worked in the sales and accounting department at Ghostly International
Records (I'm sure the founder, Sam Valenti IV, would be happy to serve as a
reference). I also worked as a bookkeeper and marketing consultant, ran a
music blog (that has since gone on hiatus), and worked for a variety of
other companies while in Uni. I also did a fair bit of booking for a
nightclub called Necto in Ann Arbor, Michigan, and served on the
media/press crew for music festivals such as Lollapalooza, Shambhala,
Electric Forest, and many more.

After Uni, I moved to San Francisco to help some friends raise money for an
launch a MIDI guitar that teaches you how to play called the Incident gTar
<https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/incident/gtar-the-first-guitar-that-anybody-can-play>.
We were wildly successful, raising $350k on Kickstarter, receiving $1
million in funding from Red Octane (the makers of Guitar Hero, who saw what
we were doing as guitar hero for actually learning how to play guitar), and
is used in the studio by the likes of Nick Thayer, Skrillex, Dirty South,
and Jason Mraz (Kimbra is also using one in her live setup currently - I'm
sure we could get a demo model for the label if you guys wanted!). Since
then, I have dabbled in quite a few startups and other projects.

I helped Noisebridge, a hackerspace frequented by the likes of Moldover in
SF raise $25,000. I'm currently wrapping up production on an IndieGoGo
campaign with my friend Asa Carson (who makes music under the moniker
'Asa') out of Bristol. I'm also currently helping Intua, the makers of the
app Beat Maker, (which they've told me Flying Lotus uses) curate content
and work out soundpack licensing contracts with artists and sound designers
for their upcoming release of Beat Maker 3 and the in-app sample store
(which, I would love to talk to some of the artists at Warp about a
soundpack or even a sort of overarching label sampler soundpack). I have
academic research on Pay What You Want revenue models and crowdfunding in
the arts which are currently under peer review to be published in
Marketing/Psychology journals, and I work excessively hard (it's not
uncommon for me to have 80, 90, even 100 hour weeks).

I think the biggest reason why you should hire me is that I would do a good
job because I would love what I do. I'm a huge Warp fan, and actually moved
from SF to London about 5 months ago pretty much solely because of how much
better the music is in London than in America. Artists like Boards of
Canada (I actually gave my cousin a vinyl of Music Has the Right to
Children for Christmas this past year), Squarepusher, Aphex Twin, Burial,
and Brian Eno were huge inspirations to me as I got into MIDI programming
and audio production, and it would be a dream come true to spend my time
helping artists like them be able to reach a larger audience.

I hope you'll consider my application, and sorry for the length of my cover
letter! This is just a really exciting opportunity!

Best,

Matthew

date: 2015-02-03
to: "Mgmt. - Jumpsuit Records" <mgmt@jumpsuitrecords.com>
subject: Re: Great idea merchandising TPA's old laptop into pins and such
msg_id: 14b519589a0f51ad
---

Hi Reuben,

Thanks for getting back to me. I think that really there are a bunch of
different ways that you could raise money, and that crowdfunding is such a
hot topic in charity fundraising because it's really quite effective at the
moment. It's also proved quite effective from my own experience in music
and the arts, which makes sense as it relies on many similar behavioral
nudges as Pay What You Want revenue models (PWYW in the music industry was
actually a major topic of my thesis - surprisingly no one has really
studied it academically apart from the Radiohead and Nine Inch Nails
releases, despite it being quite a hot topic in marketing academia with
research on programs like Panera Cares/etc).

My hypothesis in terms of the particular effectiveness of charitable
crowdfunds specifically is that they attract people who would normally give
X amount to a charity, who sees an entity crowdfunding in partnership with
them (usually through the original charity) and are willing to give 2X or
even more (research typically suggests up to 2.5X if the charity is aligned
well in it's purpose) to simultaneously donate and receive something
(generally a product) they would enjoy. We partnered with a few music
education programs on specific rewards when we were running the gTar
crowdfund, and I think we'll be doing the same for Asa for total revenue
above the goal.

Really, you could crowdfund anything - it depends on what your goals are.
Asa is doing his around an album recording, launch, and subsequent live
band tour. At gTar, we did it around a product launch in conjunction with
Tech Crunch Disrupt (the publicity definitely helped there). I thought the
merchandise launch in terms of the laptop could be cool, but there are
plenty of others. Tours, movies, albums, a video album, or even some wild
idea or delusion of grandeur. In a shoot-for-the-stars, completely
off-the-cuff direction, it could be even for something like building an
artistic retreat/community with recording studios and other arts spaces and
production facilities (the inspiration comes from a mix of being on crew at
Shambhala, the underground art community compounds in Berlin, the Zoo Labs
musician residency program in Oakland, CA, and the fact that I vaguely
remember inquiries on the TPA facebook about possible retreat/recording
locations). That's super ambitious, but wow, thinking about it? You could
have rewards be like a week stay there or something, which means basically
no cost to you on those ones, and you would basically eliminate all future
production costs for music/videos/etc... I mean that entire mission could
be charitable in and of itself as well, although isn't necessarily a
substitute for some sort of charitable partnership.

There really is no downside to running some sort of crowdfund, as you can
even run "flexible funding" campaigns where you get to keep what you raised
as long as you deliver the rewards, even if you don't raise the goal.
However, given how massively active the fanbase is, I don't think that'd
really be any problem.

Just for context, the gTar was literally just my friend Idan's idea that he
prototyped in his parents garage. Then we crowdfunded it and actually got
so much interest that it was a bit of a curse in disguise, as we were
backed up the subsequent 14 months with production and order fulfilment.
But we never thought we were going to hit our goal, let alone 3.5X that.

As for the photos, I just opened up my production laptop to find that it's
dead and that I've left my charger in the office. You can see my favourite
photos from EFF if you click here
<https://www.flickr.com/photos/100369418@N05/9525368315/in/photostream/> (that
one and the next two, but don't be fooled by the ad Flickr will probably
show you), but I'll send over higher res version and more of them when I
stop forgetting to bring my older charger home or bring my laptop to work
(should be tomorrow).

Let me know what you think - more than down to hop on skype or something
sometime and chat a bit more! I think it'd be a lot of fun and wildly
successful to run one with you guys!

Best,

Matthew

---

Hi,

I saw a post that TPA might make his non-functional old laptop into
jewellery, pins, etc on one of his facebook posts, and donate the money to
charity. Given the limited production that the materials would necessitate
and the charitable contribution aspect, I think it could be a really good
idea to crowdfund it.

I think it's the best way to release it because you can likely raise
substantially more money than through traditional sales channels,
particularly if you add a few components to the campaign. Pay What You Want
rewards, the ability to just donate a small sum of money (maybe for a free
tune?), and the charity aspect are all factors that have been proven to
bring in more revenue in crowdfunds in academic and statistical
research.I'd love to potentially help you guys set it up!

I guess a bit about me would help to understand where I'm coming from. I
always played music and played gigs, and then began working for Ghostly
International Records when I was 19. I also ran a blog on the side, which
actually reminds me, I was on photo have some sweet shots from TPA's set at
EFF2013 that I'll send along when I'm on my other laptop. I had hoped to
get you guys to maybe pay for them, but the blog has since gone on hiatus,
and I'm not doing anything with the photos, so you might as well have them
if there's anything you want to use them for.)

After Ghostly, I worked for Incident Technologies, where we built MIDI
guitars that can teach users how to play, which raised $350k on Kickstarter
<https://www.kickstarter.com/projects/incident/gtar-the-first-guitar-that-anybody-can-play>,
placed 2nd at TechCrunch Disrupt, and are used by Dirty South, Kimbra (she
actually uses one live too), Skrillex/OWSLA, Nick Thayer, and a bunch of
other artist. I've helped on quite a few other crowdfunds: Noisebridge
<https://www.indiegogo.com/projects/noisebridge-reboot-2014>, a hackerspace
in San Francisco and Asa <https://soundcloud.com/asamusicofficial>, a
musician out of Bristol (we're wrapping production and the campaign will
launch in the next week or so) who is raising funds for a studio and live
band equipment. I also researched Pay What You Want revenue models,
crowdfunding, and alternative fundraising methods in the University of
Michigan and the UC Berkeley marketing departments, and am published
academically in the field.

I'd seriously love to help you guys set this up - I think it could really
raise a huge amount of funding for charities, particularly if the right
ones are reached out to and help get the world out. Not to mention, the
data (anonymised of course) would be hugely beneficial to me in my research
on crowdfunding when I go back to do a PhD in the field.

Sorry for the essay of an email - I hope someone reads it!

Best,

Matthew

*Home Before:*

Who We Are



Atlanta Center for Gastroenterology, P.C. and Atlanta Endoscopy Center,
Ltd. has been serving the residents of Dekalb County and the greater
Atlanta community since 1982.



Our Board Certified Physicians are proud to provide our patients with the
best quality healthcare in a compassionate and cost effective manner. Our
patients are treated individually, as people, not just a number. They are
seen in a comfortable, personalized environment and treated with respect
and dignity.


Routine matters will be handled by scheduled appointment. Urgent matters
can be scheduled within 1-3 business days.



The majority of our procedures are performed in the privacy of our
state-of-the- art endoscopy suite, which is adjacent to our office.



Our physicians are active staff members with Dekalb Medical Center. They
take an active role in the care of their hospitalized patients.



Schedule an Appointment with us NOW!!



Atlanta Center for Gastroenterology, PC is currently accepting new
patients.  Please call *404.296.1986 *to schedule an appointment.



Click here  <http://www.acgaec.com/#!information/c1pna>to see what you need
to bring to your appointment and other relevant information.



If you have other specific questions, please call us at *404.296.1986* during
our office hours.



Click here  <http://www.acgaec.com/#!contact/c1gzt>to leave a message after
hours. Someone from our elite staff will get back to you at the earliest.


Our patients have access to clinical trials through the practice. We have
participated in multiple studies of illnesses related to digestive diseases
and hepatology. Anyone interested in learning about current active studies
are encouraged to call our Research Study Coordinator at (404)-296-1986.


Services

﻿﻿Our physicians are devoted to the care and treatment of disorders of the
digestive tract and liver diseases including;


*Physicians & Staff*

Our caring staff are always ready to help you



*Endoscopy Center*

OUR STATE OF THE ART ENDOSCOPY CENTER

Our Physicians perform the majority of our endoscopic procedures in the
privacy of our state-of-the-art endoscopy suite which is located adjacent
to our office.  Procedures performed include Colonoscopy, Upper Endoscopy,
and Flexible Sigmoidoscopy.


 Our Physicians continue to use the long standing tradition of conscious
sedation to provide a comfortable procedure while keeping expenses
reasonable for the patient. Our friendly and caring staff will provide
excellent care from the pre-procedure process to the recovery and discharge.


 We are a licensed and accredited Endoscopy Center and have been
established since 1994.  The facility undergoes rigorous inspections by the
Accreditation Association of Ambulatory Health Care, Inc. (AAAHC), the
State of Georgia, and Medicare. We follow the strict protocols and
guidelines set up by these associations as well as the American Society for
Gastrointestinal Endoscopy to ensure the safe care of our patients.


*Educational Links*

Here are some external educational links to help you understand the
different services and procedures that the Atlanta Center for
Gastroenterology has to offer. The majority of our endoscopic procedures
are performed in the privacy of our state-of-the-art endoscopy suite,
called the Atlanta Endoscopy Center, which is located adjacent to our
office.


General FAQs

*﻿**Insurance*

We are participating Medicare providers. We accept most insurance companies
including Aetna, BCBS, Cigna, State Health Benefit Plan, Tricare, United
Health Care, and many others. Please call the office to check on your
particular insurance plan.


We will file almost all insurance claims for you shortly after your visit.
Co-pays and co-insurance will be collected at time of service. For your
convenience, all major credit cards and personal checks are accepted as
methods of payment.

﻿

﻿If your insurance requires a referral from your primary care physician
please be sure that we have one before your first visit. If we do not have
a referral you will be asked to reschedule your appointment or pay for the
office visit up front.


 *What do I need to bring to my office visit?*

﻿Please bring your insurance card and drivers license. Additionally,  it is
recommended that you bring a list of questions to discuss with your doctor.

*Is there an appointment cancellation fee?*

﻿We do not charge cancellation fees at the Atlanta Center for
Gastroenterology or the Atlanta Endoscopy Center.

*At what age do I need to get a colonoscopy?*

﻿If you have a family history of colorectal cancer, it is advised that you
have a colonoscopy done at or before the age of 40. Otherwise, it is
advised that you have a colonoscopy at or before the age of 50.

Procedure FAQs

*How early do I need to arrive for a procedure? *

﻿In order to ensure that adequate preparation was done prior to the
procedure, we ask all patients reporting to the Atlanta Endoscopy Center to
come at least one hour before your procedure time.











*Gastroenterology Equipment*


In medicine and surgery, the branch that deals with the digestive system
and its disorders is called gastroenterology. This specialty focuses on the
diseases affecting the gastrointestinal system, which includes all organs
between the mouth and the anus along the alimentary canal. This specialty
also includes other organs that participate in digestion such as the liver,
gallbladder, pancreas as well as the vermiform appendix.



Given the complex anatomy of this system, specialized equipments are needed
for assessment, diagnosis and treatment of gastrointestinal diseases. This
discussion is about the special gastroenterology equipment used in medicine
and surgery. They are all needed for complete and effective management of a
gastroenterogic office or clinic. Some of these equipments must be
sterilized before use because they act as vehicles for disease transmission.

Please use this forum as a platform to post specific queries about a
condition you may be experiencing. Our expert physicians will try to answer
your queries and may be able to point you in the right direction as soon as
possible!

date: 2015-01-21
to: Jeremy Mountain <JeremyMountain@wolf-consultancy.co.uk>
subject: Re: Silicon Valley research
msg_id: 14b0bd3adcdd7239
---

Hi Jeremy,

I'm certainly happy to help. I do need to know what exactly you're planning
on using what I say for specifically, however (i.e. some sort of content or
just for your own purposes). To answer your questions briefly though:

Coding Clubs/Early Education: this is huge, especially in California and
specifically Silicon Valley. The founder of one of the companies I worked
for in SF, Incident <http://www.incidentgtar.com/>, was coding and building
electronics by the age of 5. A lot of schools offer programming classes for
upper level students, and quite a few in a few of the more techy states
have classes integrated into the curriculum from a very young age (mainly
in cities like SF, LA, NYC, Boston, Chicago, etc.). There are also a lot of
Hack-a-thons, usually topic focused and nearly always sponsored by a
company (and often participants efforts from the hack-a-thons end up
helping the sponsor companies if they're done correctly).

General Career Attractiveness: there are a lot of reasons why Silicon
Valley is an attractive employment destination. From the weather to the
salaries to the opportunities available for entrepreneurs , it would be
irresponsible to place a finger on just one factor. Quite a few of these
contributing factors result from the way the infrastructure for Silicon
Valley was laid in the wake of the rise of Fairchild Semiconductors. As
Silicon Valley as it currently exists is a hub for both tech-related
finance and investment as well as development, it attracts both the more
generally profit-seeking business-types, as well as people more interested
in participating in a culture of innovation or creating something new.

"Geek" Culture and the US: it's been a bit weird to witness this actually,
as I grew up a bit of a nerd, and the change happened in my late teenage
years/early 20s. Nerd culture is contributing more and more to pop culture
in the US, particularly in urban areas. It's been a slow road, which is
actually not too difficult to trace, but it's finally becoming cool to be
smart again, and all things apps and hi-tech are sexy right now. Certainly
makes funnelling kids into tech jobs much easier. However, it is important
to note that the majority of these people are men. I expect this will
change, as the popularisation of nerd culture has destigmatised women in
tech oriented jobs quite a bit, although it will probably take another 5
years or so until those kids are graduating University and entering the
workforce.

I could go on quite a bit about all of this, but need to know a bit more of
what you're looking for. Also, it might be easier to do it on Skype or on
the phone.

Best,

Matthew

date: 2015-01-16
to: Adam Benzion <adam@hackster.io>
subject: Re: Idan Beck's blog interview
msg_id: 14af2686a81e8511
---

Sorry for the delay, see below.


*Hackster: How did you get started hacking?*

Idan Beck: While I don't remember much of it now, my parents tell me that I
had an immediate affinity for computers and technology as soon as I was
able to walk and speak.  In pre-school I would choose my friends based on
whether or not they had computers at home, and I would go to the mall as
often as I could to see all of the new video game systems.  I was born in
Israel and only moved to the US when I was 5, so in the mid 80s when I was
still living in Israel I was seeing Amiga and Commodore systems, still what
I think of as the golden age of computing where everything was still moving
slow enough to understand (somewhat).

My dad actually 'bribed' me to leave Israel.  He had gone over to the US to
set us up for a 2-3 year sting at National Semiconductor in Silicon Valley,
and when he came back he did so with a Gameboy and told me there was a
whole lot more of that where we were going.  It was what made me agree to
go, I suppose my parents have always been democratic in that way, or at
least made sure to have everyone agree on such things.

When I got to Silicon Valley in the US in 1990, I was surrounded by people
'working on computers' and my dad got us a 486 PC from work and I was
immediately off to the races and playing with programming languages like
QBasic which was installed in MSDOS, and writing little shell scripts and
things.  I don't think I really knew what I was doing, but just installing
a game like King's Quest took a bit of finagling, and I was just
so enamored by the whole thing.  I didn't start explicitly start to study
coding and programming until I was 7 or so when I started going to this ACE
summer camp, which was computer programming summer camp at Stanford and I
did that for 3-4 years.  Then when I was 10 I got really into C and playing
with the Borland compiler and the WinBGI libraries trying to make little
video games and things.

I really like the early days, when writing a pixel to the screen was just
that, and the infamous 13h mode <http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mode_13h>!

*H: What was your first full-fledged hack?*

IB: I never really understood the meaning of 'hack' since I was always one
of those kids that liked to really understand things to the nth degree and
then build at that core level.  So I was never really hacking things per
say, but I was always trying to build things.  I don't remember what my
first actual hack was, but one that stands out, for example, came from my
obsession of flying things, especially helicopters.

I remember I had just completed a unit in Physics about rotational momentum
and the conservation of it, and it dawned on me that that's all the tail
rotor of a helicopter was really doing, but what if you had a second main
rotor going in the opposite direction of equal mass?  I hustled into the
garage and scrambled through my collection of electric motors (my dad would
bring me different DC and stepper motors he'd come across, by that time I
was 15 and had a pretty big collection of 20 or 30).  I found two of the
most identical ones, had my parents drive me down to the hobby shop to buy
some model airplane rotors and I started putting together a coaxial
helicopter that was at the time powered by a drill battery.

Of course it didn't go *anywhere* because when you flip a rotor upside down
it's still pushing air the same way.  This was in 1999 or 2000 way before
all of these RC helis were around.  They do/did build coaxial helicopters
<http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Coaxial_rotors>, but we're talking actual
ones people flew in.  I had gotten a bit too excited, but then realized
that it wasn't going to be possible to procure a mirrored rotor unless I
somehow made one.  Luckily I had a stash of sheet metal that I often used
since I was obsessed with Stop Motion animation when I was younger and
would use it to make sets and later for different projects.  I fashioned a
rotor out of it, but since I didn't have much craft ability with regards to
metal I just ended up hot gluing the rotor to the motor shaft.

I put on my safety goggles, hid behind a little barricade I made in my
garage, and engaged the power.  And it worked! The helicopter took off, and
it was stable (since I had made all these adjustments with minor power and
attaching solder as counter weights here and there).  At least it stayed
aloft for a whole 5 seconds before the glue on the sheet metal rotor melted
(since it was hot glue) and the rotor shot off ricocheting all over my
garage nearly slicing my throat a few times.  It was like a scene in a
movie!

Anyways, I waited a few years and then tried it again
<https://www.hackster.io/idanbeck_and_rohit/autonomous-helicopter> (original
school project
<https://courses.cit.cornell.edu/ee476/FinalProjects/s2006/rg242/webpage/ece%20476.htm>
)

*H: What's your favorite prototyping/dev board?*

IB: I'm a huge fan of the Stellaris family of chips, ARM Cortex M3 / M4F
etc.  I guess the whole thing is getting folded into Tiva now instead,
Stellaris was a company TI acquired.  The Tiva Lauchpad is an amazing
development platform, it's only like $10! Also, the library they give you
is extensive, and they give you all the support and if you're using code
composer studio it's all these great ecosystem.  I will say that code
composer studio is too much money, but all the libraries and compilers are
free, so if you get a bit whizzy with the the compiler, your own IDE and
gdb or something you can set it all up yourself after all.  That's still on
my to do list.

I'm also a big fan of Atmel of course, but I really don't like Arduino.  I
get that it's really convenient, and it definitely helps get people into
working with embedded systems.  But the real charm of embedded systems for
me is that you can screw things up, and that you're programming hardware
directly.  It's a lot like those early days in the 80s and 90s, where you
can switch registers, you're actually controlling how electrons are moving
and what everything is doing.  There are no layers of abstraction other
than those you create, and that to me is the ultimate creativity of
engineering!

I've got a bunch of other development systems surrounding me, but lately I
don't really use them much other than for proof of concept projects or
ideas.  For the most part, I'll start off developing the system I'm looking
to build at the schematic level, and while I'll refer to the dev board
documentation for how to schem out the system I'm building, I tend to start
to like to work directly on the system that I've built rather than a
prototype.   Although, sometimes I'll use the dev system just to make sure
code works and that there's no hardware bugs.

*H: Do you have a favorite hack or hack that inspired you?*

IB: Like I mentioned above, I really got inspired by the early valley.  I
can't really point to a single hack or anything like that, but I remember
when I was a kid you would go over to your friend's house and their dad was
in the garage tinkering with something.  I'd go over and ask what they were
doing and their response was always like, "what?! you're interested in
technology?! Here let me show you what this is all about!" and they'd let
me play with stuff and look around.

My friends were always like "my dad is so lame, I hate computers they're so
nerdy!"  It's funny how all those friends have now come back to tech since
there's a lot of money in it.  I like to think that the passion pool has
stayed the same though, lots more people doing it, but the same
distribution of people doing it because it's what they dream about at night
(true story, the other night I woke up and hurried to jot down a mechanical
- schematic I was dreaming through in my sleep - it's also happened once or
twice with music).

*H: When did you get started in music?*

IB: My parents got me into music really early.  It wasn't as much by my own
volition, but they enrolled my in piano classes when I was as young as 3.
According to them they took me to some expert and had me tested for
'absolute pitch'.  I apparently could match tones perfectly with, or
without a reference, but I'm still no good at identifying them into note
names - this was pretty useful later when I got into jazz and would be able
to play things back on the guitar on a first listen.

I didn't really get excited about music until I was 10.  My parents got me
a guitar and I started taking classical guitar lessons.  Later in high
school I really got into Jazz and rock, and was even playing paid gigs with
my high school jazz combo.  I was always really interested in electronic
music, and also got into DJing and attempted to get into production and
synthesis when I was in high school, both things that were a bit above my
pay grade.

It was in college that I really started to get into electronic music
production since all this software was now around, and PCs were strong
enough to run it.  It was a really exciting time in that sense, finally
being able to do something that I've only managed to scratch the surface
with in the past since the technology was advancing at such a rapid pace!

*H: What was your first music hack (if not the same as first full-fledged
hack)?*

IB: I recall I was playing with sound and music a fair amount in high
school as far as software and playing with it, but I can't pinpoint any
project that was specifically music/sound oriented back then.  I was a bit
of a crazy and ambitious student, and freshman year when I got to college I
immediately enrolled in senior and graduate level computer science classes
since I thought I was already a good programmer.  I took this class that
involved building a complete computer game, and one of my projects there
was a pretty hefty audio project.

This was around 2003 and actually kind of a new idea.  The idea was that
when you were playing a video game, instead of listening to a static song
playing, instead the different parts of the soundtrack were being played
independently and the overall energy level in the game would manipulate
these different parts such that the soundtrack would evolve and change,
being programmatically altered due to in-game events.   I did a lot of
other stuff for the game too, like writing the original 3D engine for
example, but the music engine was a really interesting project that really
attempted to do some new things.

I only have done two really music specific hacks to be honest.  The
technology that later became the gTar was the second!

*H: Can you tell us a bit about how you came to create the gTar?*

IB: The gTar was slightly born out of necessity, or what I call
nerd-induced necessity, which is not really necessity at all, but really an
exploration to solve an inconsequential problem that took more time than
the problem originally was wasting.  Regardless, it led to some great
things!

When I was in college, I helped a friend of mine convert his cafe into a
bar and then later a full fledged nightclub.  I took on two DJ nights on
Friday night and Saturday night.  That spot, Pixel, is now one of the last
standing bar/nightclubs in Ithaca so I'm pretty proud of that time.  I was
a really aggressive student too, so I would literally amble in around 9 pm or
so directly from lab, DJ until 1 or 2 depending on the crowd, then get back
to lab until the next night where I did it again and then came back home to
crash for a little while then finished off some more time in lab on Sunday
afternoon-evening.

I was an avid guitar player, and I was trying to maintain playing guitar,
working on technology things and being in lab all the time.  One day, in a
weird way I said to myself, maybe if I found a way to use my guitar in a
MIDI-controller way I could use it to DJ and then that way when I'm DJing
I'm also practicing guitar!  I saved up for a few weeks or eating nothing
but pasta so I could buy a Roland MIDI system to stick on to my guitar and
was very soon dissapointed to find that it was extremely inconsistent.  It
would work once in a while, but the problem is that the latency was
arbitrary and never the same.  Sometimes it would get the note right, but
there was no good way to predict it.

I went into lab, the iPhone had come out and cap-sense touch systems were
all the rage with multi-touch and all of that.  I thought to myself that
the guitar fretboard looked a lot like a matrix, and could be implemented
with such a multi-touch surface, but I wanted to make sure that we could
maintain the feel of the strings and the overall tangible level of a real
guitar, and so I put together a kind of bread board prototype with
suspended wires to act as strings and contacts to act as frets and realized
it was all very much feasible.

I sort of proved my point there, realized I didn't have the budget to build
it into a real prototype and I also had my masters thesis to work on!  So I
just put it in the closet for a while.  Later I had joined up at Microsoft
and found myself under-engaged so I started coming home and spending time
in my garage working on various projects, and that was one that came out of
the closet and got some attention among others!

*H: What was the hardest part about bringing the gTar to market? *

IB: One of the most difficult parts of getting the gTar to market was
actually getting enough of them to work.  The gTar is a pretty
sophisticated piece of technology, but it's wrapped up as this consumer
oriented product.  No one really knows, or cares, what's going on under the
hood and there certainly is a lot!  On the one hand that complexity is
beneficial in that it's really difficult to copy us, for example a
competitor of ours that launched just 3-6 months later was more than 18
months later to market and their product is less than half as long (only 5
frets) and the string length actually has a lot to do with the complexity.
They also made certain design decisions that we were refusing to make, we
wanted the gTar to look and feel like a real guitar - almost to a point of
pain since we get a lot of customers thinking they're just buying a
traditional guitar.

So in short, QA, and we did and still often do check every single unit that
was going out.  I've tuned up hundreds of guitars, strung up a countless
number as well.  But in a way, it's a nice way to slow down and make sure
you know what you're getting out to people.

I think the hardest thing, in the end of the day though, is the space that
you're in.  For example, we're ultimately in the music space.  I didn't
really think about it that way early on, but it definitely is the space
that we're in, and you're also going to be judged by other companies and
products in that space.  So the gTar gets compared to fender, and our
company gets compared to Spotify and all of their troubles and ultimate
market opportunity.

I suppose all of that has more to do with building a company than a
product, but the two are obviously related.  There are many recent examples
of hardware companies that got acquired before even launching a product,
and the value placed on those companies was a huge multiple of even the
projected sales over a decade.  On the other hand, there are companies that
are selling buttloads of crap, but that's all it is - just crap - and so
there's not much company value there, but I won't lie that I'm envious of
such a predictable cashflow!

So that's also been hard.  We're a true technology company building in a
space where there isn't really much technology, and in general there's not
a lot of value in general since it's music and it's not 3D printers or new
general purpose input/interface devices.  It's also hard when someone says
"oh, cool guitar!" when you know it's not really that and no matter how
much you try to explain it, it's still going to be just a guitar to
people.  I'm sure a lot of products fall into that.

*H: What advice would you give to hardware hackers looking to launch a
product of their own?*

IB: I'd say start sprinting as soon as possible.  Get a prototype working,
even if it looks like garbage, and hardly works.  I'd even suggest creating
a software demo if the prototype is ultimately too complex to work well, or
too costly for example.  Even if it's at a surface level, just figure out
how to do certain things like the Industrial Design and basic firmware.  If
you need help, down the line you can get it, but it'll be hard to get the
right kind of help from the right kinds of people if you can't really
represent what you're building the right way.

It's rare that you'll be able to really be able to communicate what it is
that you're trying to do. Second, because nearly all products today have
corresponding software or cloud components, having an initial physical
product/prototype is a quick way to bootstrap the development of those
components which are going to be critical to the reception of your idea.
There is this kind of limbo when first starting on a hardware project in
that sense, something has to give and if you've got a hardware and software
guy in the same room they'll argue until next year who has to get something
done first.  At the time I was both people, so I just sort of figured it
out, but since I think there's a bit more nobility in hardware (since
there's no software without hardware) it's the responsibility of the
hardware side to bit the bullet and set the path.

I feel like the above is doubly true since without the hardware the
software can't really do much.  At the end of the day, no matter how much
code you write, you can't write software that will physically move matter
around, or shoot electronics to different places unless that hardware is
already built out and ready to enable such functionality.

*H: What has Incident been working on recently?*

IB: We're definitely in the midst of some exciting things recently.  We're
in the process of developing a new hardware product that is similar to the
gTar, but is a different instrument.  Shouldn't be too hard for anyone to
guess what it might be, but we've been developing some really novel
technology that will make it a truly unique product that will also benefit
from our existing application and content ecosystem.

Separately, we're working very hard to push a lot more on the software and
platform side of things.  Up until now, while cloud powered, all of our
apps have not been able to work together in any appreciable way.  However,
with the upcoming launch of our rebuilt platform not only will it be
possible for all of the apps and devices to play together nicely, but it
will also be possible to use the platform directly through the browser,
including a way to create content right there in your browser without
having to download or install anything.  This will be really exciting,
since then you'll be able to write a song, and then learn how to play it on
the gTar or other instruments we build, across a variety of different
application experiences!

your end.

Hey Kasper,

Just wanted to send you along a list and description of the various
supporting content we'll need for the IndieGoGo campaign. Some of the stuff
I could do (I'm solid with Photoshop and Final Cut), but from what Asa has
said, I think you'd be better at this than me, and would be able to more
easily keep things branded consistently. I know you have a lot on your
plate with the main video though, which is top priority, so anyway I can
help on the supporting content production end, let me know.

We should also chat at some point about hosting the campaign video (Vimeo
vs. YouTube) at some point. It sounds like you're partial to Vimeo, and I
to YouTube. Really, I'm fine with either as long as Vimeo allows for
annotation links, but we should hash out the benefits and downsides to each
and solidify the hosting plan.

*Video Shorts*: this is really more at your discretion from the creative
standpoint, but having some short form videos would be awesome. One 15
second video for instagram (probably instrumental), one minute or 2 minute
video for social media distribution that communicates the idea of the
campaign (has Asa talking a bit about his background and the purpose of the
IGG. Any others would be gravy - if you have any ideas, I'd love to hear
them.

*Images*: So this one is probably going to be a bit of a staged process. We
need images for the IndieGoGo page and the social pages. The images for
social probably will NOT go on the IGG, but images from the IGG will likely
go on social pages. The best way to do this might be as templates with
Lorem Ipsum, which I can then write the text for. I think the way that the
"Trapt" campaign does this (see below) really well and that something
similar would work for Asa, as we don't have artwork or images of the
rewards at this point (and I was thinking using screencaptures from the
video as the backgrounds could be cool as well). I'm certainly open to
suggestions though. This one is pretty important, and can easily be
repurposed for social pages.

*Graphic Text*: It sounded like you had an idea to make the section header
text of the IGG campaign graphic text, unless I misinterpreted? I think
this would be pretty badass. If the text is editable, you can just send me
some Lorem Ipsum and I can edit them. If it's not, I can try to have all
the section heading titles finalised by mid-week so you can generate them.
Let me know what you'd prefer.

Some examples of IndieGoGo's that have done the images/graphic text well:
https://www.indiegogo.com/projects/join-miles-ahead-a-don-cheadle-film/x/8073218
https://www.indiegogo.com/projects/trapt-become-part-of-our-dna/x/8073218

Just let me know what of the above you can handle, you want to discuss, or
I should handle, and if you have any other ideas! More than down to hop on
a skype call or something to get into more details.

Best,

Matthew

Hey Naomi,

Hope all is well and that the Reboot at Noisebridge went swimmingly!

date: 2015-01-07
to: Michael Rausher <Michael.Rausher@owen.vanderbilt.edu>
subject: Follow up
msg_id: 14ac63565fddc67f
---

Hey man,

I'll address your notes from the emails all in one go here. It's a long day
and this gets a bit technical, so excuse any ridiculous jargon or just
ridiculousness.

1. Content marketing: I don't want to get too far into talking about that,
as that's what I'm paid to strategise on and I can't do it all for free!
But yeah, white papers, videos, website copy, blog, even a little social
(not what you'd think though) are some things you guys could benefit from.

2. SEO: this is very related to the above point. How long ago did your guy
finish? You need to wait at least 6 months to see the true results from an
SEO overhaul, so probably leave it for the most part. However, as you
create content, you can individually target each piece for different SEO
keywords. This includes copywriting for your site's pages, and yes,
duplicating keywords. However, you do need to avoid "keyword stuffing" else
suffer the wrath of the Google. But ultimately, content is MORE important
than SEO as it actually precludes it. I can certainly SEO optimise your
existing site copy and take a look over the SEO though if you'd like. This
is a bit outside the scope of running an paid digital campaign, though. I
can certainly tell you a bit more if you want to via skype though.

3. Narrowing keywords: AdWords does this automatically for about half. Then
I'll dig through them and pick out any I don't like and add negative
keywords (terms we WON'T show up for), then I'll A/B test them by running
ads. That last step is a gradual process, the most important step, and one
that has to be done AFTER the campaign has started (hence the larger spend
on the first day).

4. Ad Networks: SEM is solely google searches. There are other places
online that also use text ads in the same format as SEM, which fall into
Display Network. You would need content before you can run full Display
Network campaign, though. Google picks the sites automatically based on
your ad copy's and link destination's SEO.

5. Targeting: you can target by zip code. The preliminary research I was
doing was based on a list including "Avondale Estates, Decatur, Druid
Hills, Atlanta, Dekalb, Fulton, Georgia, Atlanta, GA, ATL, Morningside,"
etc. I'll actually look at a map when I'm putting the campaign together so
I don't miss anything, as it's been a while since I've been in Atlanta. The
varied location scopes allow for the easy testing to see which is most
effective, hence the 4 campaigns. We can also target the campaigns based on
the scope (i.e. searchers from "Georgia" and searchers from "Morningside"
see different ad copies. I think Bing might actually let you target a bit
better, but Bing is only 15% of search traffic, so we can discuss that
after these campaigns are launched.

6. The Button of Doom: I can create a dynamic link. When someone books an
appointment, are they asked where they heard about you?  A digital system
would probably be a good differentiating factor for potential patients, and
better for conversion tracking. For the email, all we need to do is have a
specific subject line, and then you can just search your email client for
that subject line and see how many results come up. A bit bootleg, but eh
whatever. But if you guys are only looking for something ABSOLUTELY
measurable, then I'm going to have to be pretty adamant that this just be
an email form link, and not a phone contact button, so no point doing the
dynamic link on second thought.

7. Measurable results: small business marketing is inherently not
measurable. I get that this frustrates small business owners who aren't
marketing-minded, but that's the reality. Until literally EVERYTHING
EVERYONE does online is tracked and digital privacy does not exist, it will
be impossible to track everything, and even when that happens, you'll
probably have to pay out the ass for it. There's a principle in marketing
and psychology research called the mere-exposure effect, which basically
can be interpreted in the context of SEM to be that the more times someone
is exposed to you, the more likely they will be to convert. Generally,
people need to be exposed to a brand at least 3 times before they even
recognise that it exists. You never know when someone could have saw or
clicked an ad during those three potential points of contact, and thus, you
can't measure it. Google tries to, but even that only holds true under
certain, not entirely common conditions. At some point, you need to be
realistic that the results of any marketing campaign are largely intangible
and riddled with human error.

8. Reference points: I'm seeing CPC of £0.03 for an international campaign
and £0.16 for a US/UK campaign, and £0.24 in Chile for my company (Touch
Surgery - show it to them if they do surgery! If they wanted to author a
module, it could be an excellent mutually beneficial content partnership).
But back to the reference points - the numbers I see for TS will likely be
lower than the ones you see (including conversions) because we're not
ecommerce or location based. However, you can't really see anything about
costs or quality of clicks until you run the campaign. There's a principle
that you should be willing to pay just below the lifetime value of a
customer to your business in order to acquire them...

9. Tracking: I looked it up: Wix does not allow for conversion tracking. At
this point, based on how adamant you've been about the necessity for this,
you go talk about how to get your site off Wix. However, even when you do,
you still won't be able to track very well. Estimates range between 65% and
25% as to how much of SEM is tangibly measurable.

Let me know what you want to do.

to: Michael Rausher <Michael.Rausher@owen.vanderbilt.edu>
subject: Re: Docs/info that will be helpful
msg_id: 14ac1fe14b4a0472
---

Hey Mike,

Please find attached my proposal. Let me know if you have any questions.
Just a couple of points though:

-Point 3 on "mikes brainstomed keywords" is HUGE. Content can have a much
higher ROI than SEM, and inherently improves SEO and paid (video ads, image
ads, etc.) ROI. This is frankly more important than SEM, but marketing
isn't about doing one thing flawlessly, it's about doing everything well.
-The keyword list will be massive by the time it's done and we'll narrow it
quickly.
-Don't worry about ad copy, that's the easy part and we'll A/B test the
shit out of it.
-You can't target via radius or age yet (there are experimental settings,
but only available on video ads currently).
-I haven't used Bing's ad servicing platform, but if we like the result
from the SEM, I can look into it.
-The button to schedule an appointment should open an email with trackable
sujbect line or should be a link to the phone number with a conditional
link (mobile users given phone number, desktop users sent to email client).
This is the most important measurement point on your site - I want to hear
about how you do so.
-The "user journey" as we call it, is very convoluted and likely long (in
comparison to a click to donate site where it's 2 or 3 clicks, whereas this
is likely something that is researched on many different occasions by
potential patients). You need to realise that a large portion of the
results from these ad will likely be intangible, particularly with the
conversion tracking and user flow you currently have set up.

Matthew
